<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Cloud</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cloud_(disambiguation)" title="Cloud (disambiguation)">Cloud (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Visible mass of liquid droplets or frozen crystals suspended in the atmosphere</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

.navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Weather" title="Template:Weather"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Weather" title="Template talk:Weather"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Weather&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, a <b>cloud</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> consisting of a visible mass of miniature <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> <a href="/wiki/Drop_(liquid)" title="Drop (liquid)">droplets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_crystals" title="Ice crystals">frozen crystals</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particles</a> suspended in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planetary</a> body or similar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a> or various other chemicals may compose the droplets and crystals. On <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, clouds are formed as a result of saturation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> when it is cooled to its <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a>, or when it gains sufficient <a href="/wiki/Moisture" title="Moisture">moisture</a> (usually in the form of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>) from an adjacent source to raise the dew point to the ambient <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>.
</p><p>They are seen in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Homosphere" title="Homosphere">homosphere</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a>. <b>Nephology</b> is the science of clouds, which is undertaken in the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_physics" title="Cloud physics">cloud physics</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>.  There are two methods of naming clouds in their respective layers of the homosphere, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">common name</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">Genus</a> types in the troposphere, the atmospheric layer closest to Earth's surface, have <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> names because of the universal adoption of <a href="/wiki/Luke_Howard" title="Luke Howard">Luke Howard</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a> that was formally proposed in 1802.  It became the basis of a modern international system that divides clouds into five physical <i>forms</i> which can be further divided or classified into <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> <i>levels</i> to derive ten basic <i>genera</i>. The main representative cloud types for each of these forms are <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">stratiform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumuliform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stratocumulus_cloud" title="Stratocumulus cloud">stratocumuliform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_cloud" title="Cumulonimbus cloud">cumulonimbiform</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirriform</a>.  <i>Low-level</i>  clouds do not have any altitude-related prefixes.  However <i>mid-level</i> stratiform and stratocumuliform types are given the prefix <i>alto-</i> while <i>high-level</i> variants of these same two forms carry the prefix <i>cirro-</i>.  In both cases, <i>strato-</i> is dropped from the latter form to avoid double-prefixing. Genus types with sufficient vertical extent to occupy more than one level do not carry any altitude related prefixes.  They are classified formally as low- or mid-level depending on the altitude at which each initially forms, and are also more informally characterized as <i>multi-level</i> or <i>vertical</i>.   Most of the ten genera derived by this method of <a href="/wiki/Classification" title="Classification">classification</a> can be subdivided into <i>species</i> and further subdivided into <i>varieties</i>. Very low stratiform clouds that extend down to the Earth's surface are given the common names <i>fog</i> and <i>mist</i>, but have no Latin names.
</p><p>In the stratosphere and mesosphere, clouds have common names for their main types. They may have the appearance of stratiform veils or sheets, cirriform wisps, or stratocumuliform bands or ripples. They are seen infrequently, mostly in the polar regions of Earth. Clouds have been observed in the atmospheres of other <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and beyond. However, due to their different temperature characteristics, they are often composed of other substances such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, as well as water.
</p><p>Tropospheric clouds can have a direct effect on <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> on Earth.  They may reflect incoming rays from the sun which can contribute to a cooling effect where and when these clouds occur, or trap longer wave radiation that reflects back up from the Earth's surface which can cause a warming effect.  The altitude, form, and thickness of the clouds are the main factors that affect the local heating or cooling of Earth and the atmosphere.  Clouds that form above the troposphere are too scarce and too thin to have any influence on climate change. Clouds are the main uncertainty in <a href="/wiki/Climate_sensitivity" title="Climate sensitivity">climate sensitivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Distribution:_Where_tropospheric_clouds_are_most_and_least_prevalent"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Distribution: Where tropospheric clouds are most and least prevalent</span></a>

</ul>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Luminance,_reflectivity,_and_coloration"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Luminance, reflectivity, and coloration</span></a></li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tabular_overview">Tabular overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Tabular overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The table that follows is very broad in scope like the cloud template that follows it. There are some variations in styles of nomenclature between the classification scheme used for the troposphere (strict Latin except for surface based aerosols) and the higher levels of the homosphere (common terms, some informally derived from Latin). However, the schemes presented here share a cross-classification of physical forms and altitude levels to derive the 10 tropospheric genera,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identification_guide_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-identification_guide-4">[4]</a></sup> the fog and mist that forms at surface level, and several additional major types above the troposphere. The cumulus genus includes four species that indicate vertical size which can affect the altitude levels.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_history_of_cloud_science_and_nomenclature">Etymology and history of cloud science and nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology and history of cloud science and nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_meteorology" title="Timeline of meteorology">Timeline of meteorology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origin of the term "cloud" can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> words <i>clud</i> or <i>clod</i>, meaning a hill or a mass of stone. Around the beginning of the 13th century, the word came to be used as a metaphor for rain clouds, because of the similarity in appearance between a mass of rock and cumulus heap cloud. Over time, the metaphoric usage of the word supplanted the Old English <i><span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">weolcan</i></span></i>, which had been the literal term for clouds in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle">Aristotle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Aristotle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ancient cloud studies were not made in isolation, but were observed in combination with other <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> elements and even other natural sciences. Around 340 BC, Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorologica" title="Meteorologica">Meteorologica</a></i>, a work which represented the sum of knowledge of the time about natural science, including weather and climate. For the first time, precipitation and the clouds from which precipitation fell were called meteors, which originate from the Greek word <i>meteoros</i>, meaning 'high in the sky'. From that word came the modern term <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, the study of clouds and weather. <i>Meteorologica</i> was based on intuition and simple observation, but not on what is now considered the scientific method. Nevertheless, it was the first known work that attempted to treat a broad range of meteorological topics in a systematic way, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrological_cycle" title="Hydrological cycle">hydrological cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meteorologica_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meteorologica-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_comprehensive_classification">First comprehensive classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First comprehensive classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_types_en.svg"><img alt="g" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cloud_types_en.svg/300px-Cloud_types_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cloud_types_en.svg/450px-Cloud_types_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cloud_types_en.svg/600px-Cloud_types_en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_types_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tropospheric cloud classification by altitude of occurrence: Multi-level and vertical genus-types not limited to a single altitude level include nimbostratus, cumulonimbus, and some of the larger cumulus species.</div></div></div>
<p>After centuries of speculative theories about the formation and behavior of clouds, the first truly scientific studies were undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Luke_Howard" title="Luke Howard">Luke Howard</a> in England and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> in France. Howard was a methodical observer with a strong grounding in the Latin language, and used his background to formally classify the various tropospheric cloud types during 1802. He believed that scientific observations of the changing cloud forms in the sky could unlock the key to weather forecasting.
</p><p>Lamarck had worked independently on cloud classification the same year and had come up with a different naming scheme that failed to make an impression even in his home country of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> because it used unusually descriptive and informal French names and phrases for cloud types. His system of nomenclature included 12 categories of clouds, with such names as (translated from French) hazy clouds, dappled clouds, and broom-like clouds. By contrast, Howard used universally accepted Latin, which caught on quickly after it was published in 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-12">[12]</a></sup> As a sign of the popularity of the naming scheme, German dramatist and poet <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> composed four poems about clouds, dedicating them to Howard.
</p><p>An elaboration of Howard's system was eventually formally adopted by the International Meteorological Conference in 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-12">[12]</a></sup> This system covered only the tropospheric cloud types.  However, the discovery of clouds above the troposphere during the late 19th century eventually led to the creation of separate classification schemes that reverted to the use of descriptive common names and phrases that somewhat recalled Lamarck's methods of classification.  These very high clouds, although classified by these different methods, are nevertheless broadly similar to some cloud forms identified in the troposphere with Latin names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_in_the_homosphere:_How_air_becomes_saturated">Formation in the homosphere: How air becomes saturated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Formation in the homosphere: How air becomes saturated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cloud_physics" title="Cloud physics">Cloud physics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg/220px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg/330px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg/440px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis_clouds_in_Ukraine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cumulus_humilis_cloud" title="Cumulus humilis cloud">Cumulus humilis clouds</a> in May</div></div></div>
<p>Terrestrial clouds can be found throughout most of the homosphere, which includes the troposphere, stratosphere, and mesosphere. Within these layers of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, air can become saturated as a result of being cooled to its <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a> or by having moisture added from an adjacent source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-13">[13]</a></sup> In the latter case, saturation occurs when the dew point is raised to the ambient air temperature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adiabatic_cooling">Adiabatic cooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Adiabatic cooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate#Dry_adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">Adiabatic cooling</a> occurs when one or more of three possible lifting agents – convective, cyclonic/frontal, or orographic – cause a parcel of air containing invisible water vapor to rise and cool to its dew point, the temperature at which the air becomes saturated. The main mechanism behind this process is adiabatic cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adiabatic_process_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-adiabatic_process-14">[14]</a></sup> As the air is cooled to its dew point and becomes saturated, water vapor normally condenses to form cloud drops. This condensation normally occurs on <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">cloud condensation nuclei</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> or dust particles that are small enough to be held aloft by normal <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">circulation</a> of the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_drops_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_drops-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="36" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv" height="169" id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv/300px--Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="300"><source data-bandwidth="347088" data-framerate="30" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="540832" data-framerate="30" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of cloud evolution from cumulus humilis to cumulonimbus capillatus incus</div></div></div>
<p>One agent is the convective upward motion of air caused by daytime solar heating at surface level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability-15">[15]</a></sup> Airmass instability allows for the formation of cumuliform clouds that can produce showers if the air is sufficiently moist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> On moderately rare occasions, convective lift can be powerful enough to penetrate the tropopause and push the cloud top into the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tropopause_penetrations_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tropopause_penetrations-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Frontal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclonic" title="Cyclonic">cyclonic</a> lift occur when <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability" title="Atmospheric instability">stable</a> air is forced aloft at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">weather fronts</a> and around centers of <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low pressure</a> by a process called <a href="/wiki/Convergence_zone" title="Convergence zone">convergence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frontal_clouds_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-frontal_clouds-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">Warm fronts</a> associated with extratropical cyclones tend to generate mostly cirriform and stratiform clouds over a wide area unless the approaching warm airmass is unstable, in which case cumulus congestus or cumulonimbus clouds are usually embedded in the main precipitating cloud layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackerel_sky_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackerel_sky-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">Cold fronts</a> are usually faster moving and generate a narrower line of clouds, which are mostly stratocumuliform, cumuliform, or cumulonimbiform depending on the stability of the warm airmass just ahead of the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;N:207-212_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;N:207-212-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamy_Twilight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/220px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/330px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/440px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamy_Twilight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Windy evening <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a> enhanced by the Sun's angle, can visually mimic a <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a> resulting from orographic lift</div></div></div>
<p>A third source of lift is wind circulation forcing air over a physical barrier such as a <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountain</a> (<a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">orographic lift</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability-15">[15]</a></sup> If the air is generally stable, nothing more than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenticular_cap" title="Lenticular cap">lenticular cap</a> clouds form. However, if the air becomes sufficiently moist and unstable, orographic showers or <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> may appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-MT-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-adiabatic_cooling">Non-adiabatic cooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Non-adiabatic cooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Along with adiabatic cooling that requires a lifting agent, three major nonadiabatic mechanisms exist for lowering the temperature of the air to its dew point. Conductive, radiational, and evaporative cooling require no lifting mechanism and can cause condensation at surface level resulting in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fog_formation_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-fog_formation-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adding_moisture_to_the_air">Adding moisture to the air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Adding moisture to the air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several main sources of water vapor can be added to the air as a way of achieving saturation without any cooling process: <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> from surface water or moist ground,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convection_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-convection-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> precipitation or <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> from plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification:_How_clouds_are_identified_in_the_troposphere">Classification: How clouds are identified in the troposphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Classification: How clouds are identified in the troposphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a comprehensive listing of over 90 combinations of genera divided into species and subdivided into varieties with Latin etymologies, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_cloud_types" title="List of cloud types">List of cloud types</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG/220px-Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG/330px-Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG/440px-Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nimbostratus_praecipitatio.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nimbostratus cloud producing precipitation</div></div></div>
<p>Tropospheric classification is based on a hierarchy of categories with physical forms and altitude levels at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANDSAT_identification_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-LANDSAT_identification-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> These are cross-classified into a total of ten genus types, most of which can be divided into species and further subdivided into varieties which are at the bottom of the hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principles_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Principles-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_forms">Physical forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Physical forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus-fibratus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2204" data-file-width="3920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cirrus-fibratus.jpg/220px-Cirrus-fibratus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cirrus-fibratus.jpg/330px-Cirrus-fibratus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Cirrus-fibratus.jpg/440px-Cirrus-fibratus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus-fibratus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cirrus_fibratus" title="Cirrus fibratus">Cirrus fibratus</a> clouds in March</div></div></div>
<p>Clouds in the troposphere assume five physical forms based on structure and process of formation. These forms are commonly used for the purpose of satellite analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> They are given below in approximate ascending order of instability or <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convective</a> activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratiform">Stratiform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Stratiform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>Nonconvective</i> stratiform clouds appear in <i>stable</i> airmass conditions and, in general, have flat, sheet-like structures that can form at any altitude in the troposphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stratiform_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stratiform-33">[33]</a></sup> The stratiform group is divided by altitude range into the genera <a href="/wiki/Cirrostratus_cloud" title="Cirrostratus cloud">cirrostratus</a> (high-level), <a href="/wiki/Altostratus_cloud" title="Altostratus cloud">altostratus</a> (mid-level), <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">stratus</a> (low-level), and <a href="/wiki/Nimbostratus_cloud" title="Nimbostratus cloud">nimbostratus</a> (multi-level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> Fog is commonly considered a surface-based cloud layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-MT-22">[22]</a></sup> The fog may form at surface level in clear air or it may be the result of a very low stratus cloud subsiding to ground or sea level. Conversely, low stratiform clouds result when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advection_fog" title="Advection fog">advection fog</a> is lifted above surface level during breezy conditions.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg/220px-Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg/330px-Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg/440px-Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloudy_Skies_in_Orange_County.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stratocumulus over Orange County.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cirriform">Cirriform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cirriform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cirriform clouds in the troposphere are of the genus <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirrus</a> and have the appearance of detached or semimerged filaments. They form at high tropospheric altitudes in air that is mostly stable with little or no convective activity, although denser patches may occasionally show buildups caused by <i>limited</i> high-level <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> where the air is partly <i>unstable</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrus_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrus-34">[34]</a></sup> Clouds resembling cirrus, cirrostratus, and cirrocumulus can be found above the troposphere but are classified separately using common names.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratocumuliform">Stratocumuliform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Stratocumuliform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Clouds of this structure have both cumuliform and stratiform characteristics in the form of rolls, ripples, or elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identification_guide_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-identification_guide-4">[4]</a></sup> They generally form as a result of <i>limited convection</i> in an otherwise mostly stable airmass topped by an inversion layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Multi-regime_convection_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Multi-regime_convection-35">[35]</a></sup> If the inversion layer is absent or higher in the troposphere, increased airmass instability may cause the cloud layers to develop tops in the form of turrets consisting of embedded cumuliform buildups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-castellanus_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-castellanus-36">[36]</a></sup> The stratocumuliform group is divided into <a href="/wiki/Cirrocumulus_cloud" title="Cirrocumulus cloud">cirrocumulus</a> (high-level, <i>strato-</i> prefix dropped), <a href="/wiki/Altocumulus_cloud" title="Altocumulus cloud">altocumulus</a> (mid-level, strato- prefix dropped), and <a href="/wiki/Stratocumulus_cloud" title="Stratocumulus cloud">stratocumulus</a> (low-level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identification_guide_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-identification_guide-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1141" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg/220px-Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg/330px-Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg/440px-Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stratocumulus_stratiformis_opacus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stratocumulus cloud</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumuliform">Cumuliform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cumuliform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cumuliform clouds generally appear in isolated heaps or tufts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stommel91_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stommel91-38">[38]</a></sup> They are the product of localized but generally <i>free-convective</i> lift where no inversion layers are in the troposphere to limit vertical growth. In general, small cumuliform clouds tend to indicate comparatively weak instability. Larger cumuliform types are a sign of greater atmospheric instability and convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mossop632-634_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mossop632-634-39">[39]</a></sup> Depending on their vertical size, clouds of the <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumulus</a> genus type may be low-level or multi-level with moderate to towering vertical extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis.jpg"><img alt="Cumulonimbus Incus in dissipating stage. Observed in Florida" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cumulus_humilis.jpg/220px-Cumulus_humilis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cumulus_humilis.jpg/330px-Cumulus_humilis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cumulus_humilis.jpg/440px-Cumulus_humilis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cumulus humilis clouds</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulonimbiform">Cumulonimbiform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cumulonimbiform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston,_Texas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3130" data-file-width="4726" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg/220px-Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg/330px-Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg/440px-Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston%2C_Texas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulonimbus_calvus_cloud_over_the_Gulf_of_Mexico_in_Galveston,_Texas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cumulonimbus cloud over the Gulf of Mexico in Galveston, Texas</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">Atmospheric convection</a></div>
<p>The largest free-convective clouds comprise the genus <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_cloud" title="Cumulonimbus cloud">cumulonimbus</a>, which has a towering vertical extent. They occur in highly unstable air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-humidity,_saturation,_and_stability-15">[15]</a></sup> and often have fuzzy outlines at the upper parts of the clouds that sometimes include anvil tops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identification_guide_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-identification_guide-4">[4]</a></sup> These clouds are the product of very strong convection that can penetrate the lower stratosphere.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_and_genera">Levels and genera</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Levels and genera">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Weather_map" title="Weather map">Weather map</a> and <a href="/wiki/Station_model" title="Station model">Station model</a></div>
<p>Tropospheric clouds form in any of three levels (formerly called <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%A9tage" title="wikt:étage"><i>étages</i></a>) based on altitude range above the Earth's surface. The grouping of clouds into levels is commonly done for the purposes of <a href="/wiki/Cloud_atlas" title="Cloud atlas">cloud atlases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_weather_observation" title="Surface weather observation">surface weather observations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_map" title="Weather map">weather maps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWTOREAD_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWTOREAD-40">[40]</a></sup> The base-height range for each level varies depending on the latitudinal <a href="/wiki/Geographical_zone" title="Geographical zone">geographical zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> Each altitude level comprises two or three genus-types differentiated mainly by physical form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-appearance_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-appearance-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identification_guide_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-identification_guide-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard levels and genus-types are summarised below in approximate descending order of the altitude at which each is normally based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmo-cloud_classifications_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmo-cloud_classifications-42">[42]</a></sup> Multi-level clouds with significant vertical extent are separately listed and summarized in approximate ascending order of instability or convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level">High-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: High-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>High clouds form at altitudes of 3,000 to 7,600 m (10,000 to 25,000 ft) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a>, 5,000 to 12,200 m (16,500 to 40,000 ft) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">temperate regions</a>, and 6,100 to 18,300 m (20,000 to 60,000 ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> All cirriform clouds are classified as high, thus constitute a single genus <i>cirrus</i> (Ci). Stratocumuliform and stratiform clouds in the high altitude range carry the prefix <i>cirro-</i>, yielding the respective genus names <i>cirrocumulus</i> (Cc) and <i>cirrostratus</i> (Cs). If limited-resolution satellite images of high clouds are analysed without supporting data from direct human observations, distinguishing between individual forms or genus types becomes impossible, and they are collectively identified as <i>high-type</i> (or informally as <i>cirrus-type</i>, though not all high clouds are of the cirrus form or genus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Genus cirrus (Ci):</li></ul>
<dl><dd>These are mostly fibrous wisps of delicate, white, cirriform, ice crystal clouds that show up clearly against the blue sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrus_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-cirrus-34">[34]</a></sup> Cirrus are generally non-convective except castellanus and floccus subtypes which show limited convection. They often form along a high altitude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jetstream" title="Jetstream">jetstream</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jetstream_clouds_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jetstream_clouds-44">[44]</a></sup> and at the very leading edge of a frontal or low-pressure disturbance where they may merge into cirrostratus. This high-level cloud genus does not produce precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmo-cloud_classifications_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmo-cloud_classifications-42">[42]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1837" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/220px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/330px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/440px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>High <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirrus</a> upper-left merging into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirrostratus" title="Cirrostratus">cirrostratus</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirrocumulus" title="Cirrocumulus">cirrocumulus</a> upper right</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Genus cirrocumulus (Cc):</li></ul>
<dl><dd>This is a pure white high stratocumuliform layer of limited convection. It is composed of ice crystals or supercooled water droplets appearing as small unshaded round masses or flakes in groups or lines with ripples like sand on a beach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrocumulus_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrocumulus-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YDN-364_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-YDN-364-46">[46]</a></sup> Cirrocumulus occasionally forms alongside cirrus and may be accompanied or replaced by cirrostratus clouds near the leading edge of an active weather system. This genus-type occasionally produces virga, precipitation that evaporates below the base of the cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackerel_sky_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mackerel_sky-20">[20]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg"><img alt="A large field of cirrocumulus clouds in a blue sky, beginning to merge near the upper left." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/220px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/330px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/440px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A large field of cirrocumulus</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Genus cirrostratus (Cs):</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Cirrostratus is a thin nonconvective stratiform ice crystal veil that typically gives rise to halos caused by refraction of the <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sun's rays</a>. The sun and moon are visible in clear outline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrostratus_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrostratus-47">[47]</a></sup> Cirrostratus does not produce precipitation, but often thickens into altostratus ahead of a warm front or low-pressure area, which sometimes does.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altostratus_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Altostratus-48">[48]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-level">Mid-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Mid-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_(Abbottabad).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/220px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/330px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/440px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_(Abbottabad).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sunrise scene giving a shine to an altocumulus stratiformis perlucidus cloud (see also 'species and varieties')</div></div></div>
<p>Nonvertical clouds in the middle level are prefixed by <i>alto-</i>, yielding the genus names <i>altocumulus</i> (Ac) for stratocumuliform types and <i>altostratus</i> (As) for stratiform types. These clouds can form as low as 2,000 m (6,500 ft) above surface at any latitude, but may be based as high as 4,000 m (13,000 ft) near the poles, 7,000 m (23,000 ft) at midlatitudes, and 7,600 m (25,000 ft) in the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> As with high clouds, the main genus types are easily identified by the human eye, but distinguishing between them using satellite photography alone is not possible. When the supporting data of human observations are not available, these clouds are usually collectively identified as <i>middle-type</i> on satellite images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Genus altocumulus (Ac):</li></ul>
<dl><dd>This is a midlevel cloud layer of limited convection that is usually appears in the form of irregular patches or more extensive sheets arranged in groups, lines, or waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-altocumulus_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-altocumulus-49">[49]</a></sup> Altocumulus may occasionally resemble cirrocumulus, but is usually thicker and composed of a mix of water droplets and ice crystals, so the bases show at least some light-grey shading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ac-compared_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ac-compared-50">[50]</a></sup> Altocumulus can produce virga, very light precipitation that evaporates before reaching the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mid-level-clouds_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-mid-level-clouds-51">[51]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Genus altostratus (As):</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:As_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/220px-As_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/330px-As_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/440px-As_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:As_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altostratus translucidus near top of photo merging into altostratus opacus near bottom</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>Altostratus is a midlevel opaque or translucent nonconvective veil of grey/blue-grey cloud that often forms along warm fronts and around low-pressure areas. Altostratus is usually composed of water droplets, but may be mixed with ice crystals at higher altitudes. Widespread opaque altostratus can produce light continuous or intermittent precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-altostratus_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-altostratus-52">[52]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level">Low-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Low-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3468" data-file-width="4624" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg/220px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg/330px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg/440px-Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_humilis_clouds_over_Jakarta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_humilis" title="Cumulus humilis">Cumulus humilis</a> clouds over <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Low clouds are found from near the surface up to 2,000 m (6,500 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> Genus types in this level either have no prefix or carry one that refers to a characteristic other than altitude. Clouds that form in the low level of the troposphere are generally of larger structure than those that form in the middle and high levels, so they can usually be identified by their forms and genus types using satellite photography alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Galapagos.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/220px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/330px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/440px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Galapagos.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galapagos" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tortuga_Bay" title="Tortuga Bay">Tortuga Bay</a> (see also 'species and varieties')</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Genus stratocumulus (Sc):</li></ul>
<dl><dd>This genus type is a stratocumuliform cloud layer of limited convection, usually in the form of irregular patches or more extensive sheets similar to altocumulus but having larger elements with deeper-gray shading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratocumulus_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratocumulus-53">[53]</a></sup> Stratocumulus is often present during wet weather originating from other rain clouds, but can only produce very light precipitation on its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratocumulus-precipitation_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratocumulus-precipitation-54">[54]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species humilis – <i>little vertical extent</i>:</li></ul>
<dl><dd>These are small detached fair-weather cumuliform clouds that have nearly horizontal bases and flattened tops, and do not produce rain showers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-55">[55]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Genus stratus (St):</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/170px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/255px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/340px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stratus nebulosus translucidus</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>This is a flat or sometimes ragged nonconvective stratiform type that sometimes resembles elevated fog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratus_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratus-56">[56]</a></sup> Only very weak precipitation can fall from this cloud, usually drizzle or snow grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drizzle_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-drizzle-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snow-grains_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-snow-grains-58">[58]</a></sup> When a very low stratus cloud subsides to surface level, it loses its Latin terminology and is given the common name fog if the prevailing surface visibility is less than 1 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratus-fog_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratus-fog-59">[59]</a></sup> If the visibility is 1 km or higher, the visible condensation is termed <a href="/wiki/Mist" title="Mist">mist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mist-fog_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mist-fog-60">[60]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-level_or_moderate_vertical">Multi-level or moderate vertical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Multi-level or moderate vertical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ns1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/220px-Ns1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/330px-Ns1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/440px-Ns1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ns1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deep multi-level nimbostratus cloud covering the sky with a scattered layer of low stratus fractus pannus (see also 'species' and 'supplementary features' sections)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_Africa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2348" data-file-width="3522" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/220px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/330px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/440px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_Africa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_humilis" title="Cumulus humilis">Cumulus humilis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_mediocris" title="Cumulus mediocris">cumulus mediocris</a> with stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus in the foreground (see also 'species and varieties')</div></div></div>
<p>These clouds have low- to mid-level bases that form anywhere from near the surface to about 2,400 m (8,000 ft) and tops that can extend into the mid-altitude range and sometimes higher in the case of nimbostratus.
</p>
<ul><li>Genus nimbostratus (Ns); <i>multi-level</i>:</li></ul>
<p>This is a diffuse, dark grey, multi-level stratiform layer with great horizontal extent and usually moderate to deep vertical development that looks feebly illuminated from the inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimbostratus_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-nimbostratus-61">[61]</a></sup> Nimbostratus normally forms from mid-level altostratus, and develops at least moderate vertical extent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-63">[63]</a></sup> when the base subsides into the low level during precipitation that can reach moderate to heavy intensity. It  achieves even greater vertical development when it simultaneously grows upward into the high level due to large-scale frontal or cyclonic lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-64">[64]</a></sup> The <i>nimbo-</i> prefix refers to its ability to produce continuous rain or snow over a wide area, especially ahead of a warm front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precipitating_clouds_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-precipitating_clouds-65">[65]</a></sup> This thick cloud layer lacks any towering structure of its own, but may be accompanied by embedded towering cumuliform or cumulonimbiform types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimbostratus_associated_with_deep_convection_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-nimbostratus_associated_with_deep_convection-66">[66]</a></sup> Meteorologists affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> (WMO) officially classify nimbostratus as mid-level for synoptic purposes while informally characterizing it as multi-level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup> Independent meteorologists and educators appear split between those who largely follow the WMO model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-63">[63]</a></sup> and those who classify nimbostratus as low-level, despite its considerable vertical extent and its usual initial formation in the middle altitude range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classification_of_clouds_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-classification_of_clouds-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species mediocris – <i>moderate vertical extent</i>:</li></ul>
<dl><dd>These cumuliform clouds of free convection have clear-cut, medium-grey, flat bases and white, domed tops in the form of small sproutings and generally do not produce precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-55">[55]</a></sup> They usually form in the low level of the troposphere except during conditions of very low relative humidity, when the clouds bases can rise into the middle-altitude range. Cumulus mediocris is officially classified as low-level and more informally characterized as having moderate vertical extent that can involve more than one altitude level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-6">[6]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Towering_vertical">Towering vertical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Towering vertical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">Atmospheric convection</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2397" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/170px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/255px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/340px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Towering vertical cumulus congestus embedded within a layer of cumulus mediocris: Higher layer of stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus.</div></div></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1182" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/550px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/825px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/1100px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png 2x" width="550"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Progressive evolution of a single cell thunderstorm</div></div></div></div>
<p>These very large cumuliform and cumulonimbiform types have cloud bases in the same low- to mid-level range as the multi-level and moderate vertical types, but the tops nearly always extend into the high levels.  Unlike less vertically developed clouds, they are required to be identified by their standard names or abbreviations in all aviation observations (METARS) and forecasts (TAFS) to warn pilots of possible severe weather and turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species congestus – <i>great vertical extent</i>:</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Increasing airmass instability can cause free-convective cumulus to grow very tall to the extent that the vertical height from base to top is greater than the base-width of the cloud. The cloud base takes on a darker grey coloration and the top commonly resembles a cauliflower. This cloud type can produce moderate to heavy showers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-55">[55]</a></sup> and is designated <i>Towering cumulus</i> (Tcu) by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization_(ICAO)" title="International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO)">International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO)</a>.</dd></dl>
<ul><li>Genus cumulonimbus (Cb):</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2471" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/220px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/330px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/440px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isolated cumulonimbus cloud over the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, releasing a heavy shower</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>This genus type is a heavy, towering, cumulonimbiform mass of free-convective cloud with a dark-grey to nearly black base and a very high top in the form of a mountain or huge tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulonimbus_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumulonimbus-69">[69]</a></sup> Cumulonimbus can produce <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>, local very heavy downpours of <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> that may cause <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash floods</a>, and a variety of types of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> including cloud-to-ground that can cause <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2000_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2000-70">[70]</a></sup> Other convective severe weather may or may not be associated with thunderstorms and include heavy <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a> showers, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncar_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncar-71">[71]</a></sup> strong <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downbursts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renno_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renno-73">[73]</a></sup> Of all these possible cumulonimbus-related events, lightning is the only one of these that requires a thunderstorm to be taking place since it is the lightning that creates the thunder. Cumulonimbus clouds can form in unstable airmass conditions, but tend to be more concentrated and intense when they are associated with unstable <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;N:207-212_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;N:207-212-21">[21]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genus types are commonly divided into subtypes called <i>species</i> that indicate specific structural details which can vary according to the stability and windshear characteristics of the atmosphere at any given time and location. Despite this hierarchy, a particular species may be a subtype of more than one genus, especially if the genera are of the same physical form and are differentiated from each other mainly by altitude or level. There are a few species, each of which can be associated with genera of more than one physical form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-species-74">[74]</a></sup> The species types are grouped below according to the physical forms and genera with which each is normally associated. The forms, genera, and species are listed from left to right in approximate ascending order of instability or convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
