<h2><span id="Effects_on_the_troposphere.2C_climate.2C_and_climate_change"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_the_troposphere,_climate,_and_climate_change">Effects on the troposphere, climate, and climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Effects on the troposphere, climate, and climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cover" title="Cloud cover">Cloud cover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_feedback" title="Cloud feedback">Cloud feedback</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a></div>
<p>Tropospheric clouds exert numerous influences on Earth's troposphere and climate. First and foremost, they are the source of precipitation, thereby greatly influencing the distribution and amount of precipitation. Because of their differential buoyancy relative to surrounding cloud-free air, clouds can be associated with vertical motions of the air that may be convective, frontal, or cyclonic. The motion is upward if the clouds are less dense because condensation of water vapor releases heat, warming the air and thereby decreasing its density. This can lead to downward motion because lifting of the air results in cooling that increases its density. All of these effects are subtly dependent on the vertical temperature and moisture structure of the atmosphere and result in major redistribution of heat that affect the Earth's climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud-heating_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud-heating-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The complexity and diversity of clouds in the troposphere is a major reason for difficulty in quantifying the effects of clouds on climate and climate change. On the one hand, white cloud tops promote cooling of Earth's surface by reflecting shortwave radiation (visible and near infrared) from the sun, diminishing the amount of solar radiation that is absorbed at the surface, enhancing the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. Most of the sunlight that reaches the ground is absorbed, warming the surface, which emits radiation upward at longer, infrared, wavelengths. At these wavelengths, however, water in the clouds acts as an efficient absorber. The water reacts by radiating, also in the infrared, both upward and downward, and the downward longwave radiation results in increased warming at the surface. This is analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud-heating_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-cloud-heating-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>High-level genus-types particularly show this duality with both short-wave <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> cooling and long-wave greenhouse warming effects. On the whole, <i>ice-crystal</i> clouds in the upper troposphere (cirrus) tend to favor net warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nucleation_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nucleation-122">[122]</a></sup> However, the cooling effect is dominant with mid-level and low clouds, especially when they form in extensive sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-121">[121]</a></sup> Measurements by NASA indicate that on the whole, the effects of low and mid-level clouds that tend to promote cooling outweigh the warming effects of high layers and the variable outcomes associated with vertically developed clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>As difficult as it is to evaluate the influences of current clouds on current climate, it is even more problematic to predict changes in cloud patterns and properties in a future, warmer climate, and the resultant cloud influences on future climate. In a warmer climate more water would enter the atmosphere by evaporation at the surface; as clouds are formed from water vapor, cloudiness would be expected to increase. But in a warmer climate, higher temperatures would tend to evaporate clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Both of these statements are considered accurate, and both phenomena, known as cloud feedbacks, are found in climate model calculations. Broadly speaking, if clouds, especially low clouds, increase in a warmer climate, the resultant cooling effect leads to a negative feedback in climate response to increased greenhouse gases. But if low clouds decrease, or if high clouds increase, the feedback is positive. Differing amounts of these feedbacks are the principal reason for differences in <a href="/wiki/Climate_sensitivity" title="Climate sensitivity">climate sensitivities</a> of current global climate models. As a consequence, much research has focused on the response of low and vertical clouds to a changing climate. Leading global models produce quite different results, however, with some showing increasing low clouds and others showing decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> For these reasons the role of tropospheric clouds in regulating <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> remains a leading source of uncertainty in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For Global brightening, see <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>.</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_stratospheric">Polar stratospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Polar stratospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/220px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/330px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/440px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lenticular nacreous clouds over Antarctica</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polar_stratospheric_cloud" title="Polar stratospheric cloud">Polar stratospheric clouds</a> (PSC's) form in the lowest part of the stratosphere during the <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>, at the altitude and during the season that produces the coldest temperatures and therefore the best chances of triggering condensation caused by adiabatic cooling.   Moisture is scarce in the stratosphere, so nacreous and non-nacreous cloud at this altitude range is restricted to polar regions in the winter where the air is coldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>PSC's show some variation in structure according to their chemical makeup and atmospheric conditions, but are limited to a single very high range of altitude of about 15,000–25,000 m (49,200–82,000 ft), so they are not classified into altitude levels, genus types, species, or varieties. There is no Latin nomenclature in the manner of tropospheric clouds, but rather descriptive names using common English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Supercooled nitric acid and water PSC's, sometimes known as type 1, typically have a stratiform appearance resembling cirrostratus or haze, but because they are not frozen into crystals, do not show the pastel colours of the nacreous types.  This type of PSC has been identified as a cause of ozone depletion in the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSC_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSC-128">[128]</a></sup> The frozen nacreous types are typically very thin with mother-of-pearl colorations and an undulating cirriform or lenticular (stratocumuliform) appearance.  These are sometimes known as type 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nacreous_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-nacreous-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_mesospheric">Polar mesospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Polar mesospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="3402" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/220px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/330px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/440px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Noctilucent_cloud" title="Noctilucent cloud">Noctilucent cloud</a> over Estonia</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_mesospheric_cloud" title="Polar mesospheric cloud">Polar mesospheric clouds</a> form at an extreme-level altitude range of about 80 to 85 km (50 to 53 mi). They are given the Latin name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noctilucent_clouds" title="Noctilucent clouds">noctilucent</a> because of their illumination well after sunset and before sunrise. They typically have a bluish or silvery white coloration that can resemble brightly illuminated cirrus. Noctilucent clouds may occasionally take on more of a red or orange hue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-7">[7]</a></sup> They are not common or widespread enough to have a significant effect on climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simulation_studies_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-simulation_studies-131">[131]</a></sup> However, an increasing frequency of occurrence of noctilucent clouds since the 19th century may be the result of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noctilucent-cloud_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-noctilucent-cloud-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noctilucent clouds are the highest in the atmosphere and form near the top of the mesosphere at about ten times the altitude of tropospheric high clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-133">[133]</a></sup> From ground level, they can occasionally be seen illuminated by the sun during deep <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>. Ongoing research indicates that convective lift in the mesosphere is strong enough during the polar <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> to cause adiabatic cooling of small amount of water vapour to the point of saturation. This tends to produce the coldest temperatures in the entire atmosphere just below the mesopause. These conditions result in the best environment for the formation of polar mesospheric clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simulation_studies_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-simulation_studies-131">[131]</a></sup> There is also evidence that smoke particles from burnt-up meteors provide much of the condensation nuclei required for the formation of noctilucent cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sounding_rocket_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-sounding_rocket-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noctilucent clouds have four major types based on physical structure and appearance. Type I veils are very tenuous and lack well-defined structure, somewhat like cirrostratus fibratus or poorly defined cirrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-veils_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-veils-135">[135]</a></sup> Type II bands are long streaks that often occur in groups arranged roughly parallel to each other. They are usually more widely spaced than the bands or elements seen with cirrocumulus clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bands_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-bands-136">[136]</a></sup> Type III billows are arrangements of closely spaced, roughly parallel short streaks that mostly resemble cirrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billows_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-billows-137">[137]</a></sup> Type IV whirls are partial or, more rarely, complete rings of cloud with dark centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whirls_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-whirls-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Distribution in the mesosphere is similar to the stratosphere except at much higher altitudes. Because of the need for maximum cooling of the water vapor to produce noctilucent clouds, their distribution tends to be restricted to polar regions of Earth. A major seasonal difference is that convective lift from below the mesosphere pushes very scarce water vapor to higher colder altitudes required for cloud formation during the respective summer seasons in the northern and southern hemispheres. Sightings are rare more than 45 degrees south of the north pole or north of the south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial">Extraterrestrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmospheres" title="Extraterrestrial atmospheres">Extraterrestrial atmospheres</a></div>
<p>Cloud cover has been seen on most other planets in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>'s thick clouds are composed of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (due to volcanic activity) and appear to be almost entirely stratiform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bougher-127-129_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bougher-127-129-139">[139]</a></sup> They are arranged in three main layers at altitudes of 45 to 65 km that obscure the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>'s surface and can produce <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a>. No embedded cumuliform types have been identified, but broken stratocumuliform wave formations are sometimes seen in the top layer that reveal more continuous layer clouds underneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mysterious_waves_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mysterious_waves-140">[140]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, noctilucent, cirrus, cirrocumulus and stratocumulus composed of water-ice have been detected mostly near the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-photo_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-photo-142">[142]</a></sup> Water-ice fogs have also been detected on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars-clouds_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars-clouds-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> have an outer cirriform cloud deck composed of ammonia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> an intermediate stratiform haze-cloud layer made of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a>, and an inner deck of cumulus water clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Embedded cumulonimbus are known to exist near the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jupiter-clouds_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jupiter-clouds-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn-clouds_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn-clouds-149">[149]</a></sup> The same category-types can be found covering <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, but are all composed of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neptune's_Atmosphere_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neptune's_Atmosphere-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uranus-clouds_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uranus-clouds-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irwin-115_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-115-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (February 2022)">excessive citations</span></a></i>]</sup> Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> has cirrus clouds believed to be composed largely of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coustenis155_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coustenis155-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> Saturn mission uncovered evidence of polar stratospheric clouds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_on_titan_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_on_titan-158">[158]</a></sup> and a methane cycle on Titan, including lakes near the poles and fluvial channels on the surface of the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methane-cycle_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-methane-cycle-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some planets outside the Solar System are known to have atmospheric clouds. In October 2013, the detection of high altitude optically thick clouds in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> <a href="/wiki/Kepler-7b" title="Kepler-7b">Kepler-7b</a> was announced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-20131002_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-20131002-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> and, in December 2013, in the atmospheres of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_436_b" title="GJ 436 b">GJ 436 b</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_1214_b" title="GJ 1214 b">GJ 1214 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131231_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131231-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101a_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101a-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101b_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101b-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101c_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101c-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture_and_religion">In culture and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: In culture and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3409" data-file-width="5001" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/290px-Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/435px-Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/580px-Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_West_-_Joshua_passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joshua_Passing_the_River_Jordan_with_the_Ark_of_the_Covenant&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joshua Passing the River Jordan with the Ark of the Covenant (page does not exist)">Joshua Passing the River Jordan with the Ark of the Covenant</a></i> (1800) by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a>, showing <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a> leading the Israelites through the desert in the form of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Cloud" title="Pillar of Cloud">pillar of cloud</a>, as described in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Exodus#13:21" title="s:Bible (King James)/Exodus">Exodus 13:21–22</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gertz_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gertz-166">[166]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Clouds play an important mythical or non-scientific role in various cultures and religious traditions. The ancient <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadians</a> believed that the clouds (in meteorology, probably the supplementary feature <i>mamma</i>) were the breasts of the sky goddess <a href="/wiki/Antu_(goddess)" title="Antu (goddess)">Antu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nemet-Nejat_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nemet-Nejat-167">[167]</a></sup> and that rain was milk from her breasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nemet-Nejat_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nemet-Nejat-167">[167]</a></sup> In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Exodus#13:21" title="s:Bible (King James)/Exodus">Exodus 13:21–22</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a> is described as guiding the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> through the desert in the form of a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Cloud" title="Pillar of Cloud">pillar of cloud</a>" by day and a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Fire_(theophany)" title="Pillar of Fire (theophany)">pillar of fire</a>" by night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gertz_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gertz-166">[166]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uthra" title="Uthra">uthras</a> (celestial beings) are also occasionally mentioned as being in <i>anana</i> ("clouds"; e.g., in <i><a href="/wiki/Right_Ginza" title="Right Ginza">Right Ginza</a></i> Book 17, Chapter 1), which can also be interpreted as female consorts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR_Gelbert_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-GR_Gelbert-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_comedy" title="Ancient Greek comedy">ancient Greek comedy</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Clouds" title="The Clouds">The Clouds</a></i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a> and first performed at the <a href="/wiki/Dionysia" title="Dionysia">City Dionysia</a> in 423 BC, the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> declares that the Clouds are the only true deities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup> and tells the main character Strepsiades not to worship any deities other than the Clouds, but to pay homage to them alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup> In the play, the Clouds change shape  to reveal the true nature of whoever is looking at them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roche_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roche-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robson_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robson-171">[171]</a></sup> turning into <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaurs</a> at the sight of a <a href="/wiki/Long_hair" title="Long hair">long-haired</a> politician, wolves at the sight of the <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzler</a> Simon, deer at the sight of the coward <a href="/wiki/Cleonymus_of_Athens" title="Cleonymus of Athens">Cleonymus</a>, and mortal women at the sight of the effeminate <a href="/wiki/Theoroi" title="Theoroi">informer</a> <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes_(son_of_Sibyrtius)" title="Cleisthenes (son of Sibyrtius)">Cleisthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roche_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roche-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robson_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robson-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup> They are hailed the source of inspiration to comic poets and philosophers;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-4"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup> they are masters of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, regarding <a href="/wiki/Eloquence" title="Eloquence">eloquence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sophistry" title="Sophistry">sophistry</a> alike as their "friends".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss1966_169-5"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss1966-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, clouds are symbols of luck and happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ding_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ding-172">[172]</a></sup> Overlapping clouds (in meteorology, probably <i>duplicatus clouds) are thought to imply eternal happiness<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ding_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ding-172">[172]</a></sup> and clouds of different colors are said to indicate "multiplied blessings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ding_172-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ding-172">[172]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>Cloud watching or cloud gazing is a popular children's activity involving watching the clouds and looking for shapes in them, a form of <a href="/wiki/Pareidolia" title="Pareidolia">pareidolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
